<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Glock</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>

<p>The <b>Glock pistol</b>, sometimes referred to by the manufacturer as a Glock "Safe Action" pistol and colloquially as a <b>Glock</b>, is a series of <a href="/wiki/Polymer" title="Polymer">polymer</a>-<a href="/wiki/Receiver_(firearms)" title="Receiver (firearms)">framed</a>, <a href="/wiki/Recoil_operation#Short_recoil_operation" title="Recoil operation">short recoil</a>-operated, locked-breech <a href="/wiki/Semi-automatic_pistol" title="Semi-automatic pistol">semi-automatic pistols</a> designed and produced by <a href="/wiki/Glock_Ges.m.b.H." title="Glock Ges.m.b.H.">Glock Ges.m.b.H.</a>, located in <a href="/wiki/Deutsch-Wagram" title="Deutsch-Wagram">Deutsch-Wagram</a>, Austria. It entered <a href="/wiki/Austrian_Armed_Forces" title="Austrian Armed Forces">Austrian military</a> and <a href="/wiki/Federal_Police_(Austria)" title="Federal Police (Austria)">police</a> service by 1982 after it was the top performer on an exhaustive series of reliability and safety tests.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kasler_2_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kasler_2-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<p>Despite initial resistance from the market to accept a perceived "plastic gun" due to unfounded durability and reliability concerns and fears that it might circumvent <a href="/wiki/Metal_detector#Security_screening" title="Metal detector">metal detectors</a> in airports, Glock pistols have become the company's most profitable line of products, commanding 65% of the market share of handguns for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_law_enforcement" title="United States law enforcement">United States law enforcement</a> agencies, as well as supplying numerous national armed forces, security agencies, and police forces in at least 48 countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sweeney_2008_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sweeney_2008-6">[6]</a></sup> Glocks are also popular firearms among civilians for recreational and competition shooting, home and self-defense, and <a href="/wiki/Concealed_carry" title="Concealed carry">concealed carry</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Open_carry" title="Open carry">open carry</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glock&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The company's founder, <a href="/wiki/Engineer" title="Engineer">engineer</a> <a href="/wiki/Gaston_Glock" title="Gaston Glock">Gaston Glock</a>, had no experience with firearms design or manufacture at the time their first pistol, the Glock 17, was being <a href="/wiki/Prototype" title="Prototype">prototyped</a>. Glock did, however, have extensive experience in advanced synthetic <a href="/wiki/Polymer" title="Polymer">polymers</a>, knowledge of which was instrumental in the company's design of the first commercially successful line of pistols with a polymer frame.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> Glock introduced <a href="/wiki/Ferritic_nitrocarburizing" title="Ferritic nitrocarburizing">ferritic nitrocarburizing</a> into the firearms industry as an anticorrosion surface treatment for metal gun parts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MetalTreatments_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-MetalTreatments-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Development">Development</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glock&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Development">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Glock_17_9mmPara_002.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="800" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Glock_17_9mmPara_002.jpg/220px-Glock_17_9mmPara_002.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Glock_17_9mmPara_002.jpg/330px-Glock_17_9mmPara_002.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Glock_17_9mmPara_002.jpg/440px-Glock_17_9mmPara_002.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Glock_17_9mmPara_002.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A "first-generation" Glock 17 with the slide locked back displaying its vertical barrel tilt</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Glock_17_2nd_Gen.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1361" data-file-width="1983" height="151" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Glock_17_2nd_Gen.jpg/220px-Glock_17_2nd_Gen.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Glock_17_2nd_Gen.jpg/330px-Glock_17_2nd_Gen.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Glock_17_2nd_Gen.jpg/440px-Glock_17_2nd_Gen.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>

</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">

</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ARMS_%26_Hunting_2012_exhibition_(474-23).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1500" data-file-width="2250" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/ARMS_%26_Hunting_2012_exhibition_%28474-23%29.jpg/220px-ARMS_%26_Hunting_2012_exhibition_%28474-23%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/ARMS_%26_Hunting_2012_exhibition_%28474-23%29.jpg/330px-ARMS_%26_Hunting_2012_exhibition_%28474-23%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/ARMS_%26_Hunting_2012_exhibition_%28474-23%29.jpg/440px-ARMS_%26_Hunting_2012_exhibition_%28474-23%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ARMS_%26_Hunting_2012_exhibition_(474-23).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A "fourth-generation" Glock 17, identified by an enlarged magazine release catch, modified rough texture frame grip checkering, interchangeable backstraps, and a "Gen4" rollmark on the slide</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1980, the <a href="/wiki/Austrian_Armed_Forces" title="Austrian Armed Forces">Austrian military</a> announced that it would seek tenders for a new, modern duty pistol to replace their <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>-era <a href="/wiki/Walther_P38" title="Walther P38">Walther P38</a> handguns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kasler_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kasler-10">[10]</a></sup> The Austrian Ministry of Defence formulated a list of 17 criteria for the new generation service pistol:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kasler_2_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kasler_2-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li>The design was to be <a href="/wiki/Semi-automatic_firearm" title="Semi-automatic firearm">self loading</a>.</li>
<li>The pistol was to fire the <a href="/wiki/NATO" title="NATO">NATO</a>-standard <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/9%C3%9719_mm_Parabellum" title="9×19 mm Parabellum">9×19 mm Parabellum</a> round.</li>
<li>The magazines were not to require any means of assistance for loading.</li>
<li>The magazines were to have a minimum capacity of eight rounds.</li>
<li>It was to be possible to accomplish all actions one handed which are necessary to prepare the pistol for firing and any actions required after firing. <a href="/wiki/Ambidexterity" title="Ambidexterity">Right-handed as well as left-handed</a>.</li>
<li>The pistol was to be absolutely secure against <a href="/wiki/Unintentional_discharge_(firearms)" title="Unintentional discharge (firearms)">accidental discharge</a> from shock, strike, and drop from a height of 2 m onto a steel plate.</li>
<li>Disassembly of the main parts for maintenance and reassembling was to be possible without the use of any tools.</li>
<li>Maintenance and cleaning of the pistol was to be accomplished without the use of tools.</li>
<li>The pistol's construction was not to exceed 58 individual parts (equivalent of a <a href="/wiki/Walther_P38" title="Walther P38">P38</a>).</li>
<li>Gauges, measurement tools, and precise testing devices were not to be necessary for the long-term maintenance of the pistol.</li>
<li>The manufacturer was to be required to provide the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Defence_(Austria)" title="Ministry of Defence (Austria)">ministry of defence</a> with a complete set of <a href="/wiki/Engineering_drawing" title="Engineering drawing">engineering drawings</a> and exploded views. These were to be supplied with all the relevant details for the production of the pistol.</li>
<li>All components were to be fully interchangeable between pistols.</li>
<li>No more than 20 malfunctions were to be permitted during the first 10,000 rounds fired, not even minor jams that could be cleared without the use of any tools.</li>
<li>After firing 15,000-rounds of standard ammunition, the pistol was to be inspected for wear. The pistol was to then be used to fire an <a href="/wiki/Overpressure_ammunition" title="Overpressure ammunition">overpressure</a> test cartridge generating 5,000 bar (500 MPa; 73,000 psi). (The normal maximum operating pressure Pmax for the <a href="/wiki/NATO_EPVAT_testing#Proofing" title="NATO EPVAT testing">9mm NATO</a> is rated at 2,520 bar (252 MPa; 36,500 psi).)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> The critical components were to continue to function properly and be up to specifications, otherwise the pistol was to be disqualified.</li>
<li>When handled properly, under no circumstances was the user to be endangered by case ejection.</li>
<li>The muzzle energy was to be at least 441.5 <a href="/wiki/Joule" title="Joule">J</a> when firing a 9×19 mm S-round/P-08 by <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Hirtenberger_AG&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Hirtenberger AG (page does not exist)">Hirtenberger AG</a>.</li>
<li>Pistols scoring less than 70% of the total available points were not to be considered for military use.</li>
</ul>
<p>Glock became aware of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Austrian_Army" title="Austrian Army">Austrian Army</a>'s planned procurement, and in 1982 assembled a team of Europe's leading handgun experts from military, police, and civilian sport-shooting circles to define the most desirable characteristics in a combat pistol.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kasler_2_5-2"><a href="#cite_note-Kasler_2-5">[5]</a></sup> Within three months, Glock developed a working prototype that combined proven mechanisms and traits from previous pistol designs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kasler_4_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kasler_4-12">[12]</a></sup> In addition the plan was to make extensive use of synthetic materials and modern manufacturing technologies, to make it a very cost-effective candidate.</p>
<p>Several samples of the 9×19mm Glock 17 (so named because it was the 17th patent procured by the company<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup>) were submitted for assessment trials in early 1982, and after passing all of the exhaustive endurance and abuse tests, the Glock emerged as the winner.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<p>The handgun was adopted into service with the Austrian military and police forces in 1982 as the P80 (<i>Pistole 80</i>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wo.C5.BAniak_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wo.C5.BAniak-17">[17]</a></sup> with an initial order for 25,000 guns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kasler_4_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kasler_4-12">[12]</a></sup> The Glock 17 outperformed eight different pistols from five other established manufacturers (<a href="/wiki/Heckler_%26_Koch" title="Heckler &amp; Koch">Heckler &amp; Koch</a> of Germany offered their <a href="/wiki/Heckler_%26_Koch_P7" title="Heckler &amp; Koch P7">P7M8</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/HK_P7M13" title="HK P7M13">P7M13</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heckler_%26_Koch_P9S" title="Heckler &amp; Koch P9S">P9S</a>, <a href="/wiki/SIG_Sauer" title="SIG Sauer">SIG Sauer</a> of <a href="/wiki/Switzerland" title="Switzerland">Switzerland</a> bid with their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SIG_P220" title="SIG P220">P220</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SIG_P226" title="SIG P226">P226</a> models, <a href="/wiki/Beretta" title="Beretta">Beretta</a> of <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a> submitted their model <a href="/wiki/Beretta_92#History" title="Beretta 92">92SB-F</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fabrique_Nationale_de_Herstal" title="Fabrique Nationale de Herstal">FN Herstal</a> proposed an updated variant of the <a href="/wiki/Browning_Hi-Power" title="Browning Hi-Power">Browning Hi-Power</a>, and the home-grown <a href="/wiki/Steyr_Mannlicher" title="Steyr Mannlicher">Steyr Mannlicher</a> entered the competition with the <a href="/wiki/Steyr_GB" title="Steyr GB">GB</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kasler_6_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kasler_6-18">[18]</a></sup></p>
<p>The results of the Austrian trials sparked a wave of interest in Western Europe and overseas, particularly in the United States, where a similar effort to select a service-wide replacement for the <a href="/wiki/M1911_pistol" title="M1911 pistol">M1911</a> had been going on since the late 1970s (known as the <a href="/wiki/Joint_Service_Small_Arms_Program" title="Joint Service Small Arms Program">Joint Service Small Arms Program</a>). In late 1983, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Defense" title="United States Department of Defense">United States Department of Defense</a> inquired about the Glock pistol and received four samples of the Glock 17 for unofficial evaluation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kasler_7_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kasler_7-19">[19]</a></sup> Glock was then invited to participate in the XM9 Personal Defense Pistol Trials, but declined because the DOD specifications would require extensive retooling of production equipment and providing 35 test samples in an unrealistic time frame.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kasler_7_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kasler_7-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<p>Shortly thereafter, the Glock 17 was accepted into service with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Royal_Norwegian_Army" title="Royal Norwegian Army">Norwegian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Swedish_Armed_Forces" title="Swedish Armed Forces">Swedish</a> armed forces, surpassing all prior NATO durability standards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kasler_7_19-2"><a href="#cite_note-Kasler_7-19">[19]</a></sup> As a result, the Glock 17 became a standard NATO-classified sidearm and was granted a <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=NATO_stock_number&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="NATO stock number (page does not exist)">NATO stock number</a> (1005-25-133-6775).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kasler_7_19-3"><a href="#cite_note-Kasler_7-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<p>By 1992, some 350,000 pistols had been sold in more than 45 countries, including 250,000 in the United States alone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wo.C5.BAniak_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wo.C5.BAniak-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<p>Starting in 2013 the <a href="/wiki/British_Army" title="British Army">British Army</a> is replacing the <a href="/wiki/Browning_Hi-Power" title="Browning Hi-Power">Browning Hi-Power</a> pistol with the Glock 17 Gen 4, due to concerns about weight and the external safety of the Hi-Power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Product_evolution">Product evolution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glock&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Product evolution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Glock has updated its basic design several times throughout its production history. Commentators had long separated the large changes into generations. Glock eventually accepted this nomenclature with their "Gen4" models.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Second-generation_models">Second-generation models</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glock&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Second-generation models">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>A mid-life upgrade to the Glock pistols involved the addition of checkering on the front strap and serrations to the back strap. These versions, introduced in 1988, were informally referred to as "second-generation" models. To meet American <a href="/wiki/Bureau_of_Alcohol,_Tobacco,_Firearms_and_Explosives" title="Bureau of Alcohol, Tobacco, Firearms and Explosives">ATF</a> regulations, a steel plate with a stamped serial number was embedded into the receiver in front of the trigger guard.</p>
<p>In 1991, an integrated recoil spring assembly replaced the original two-piece recoil spring and tube design. The magazine was slightly modified, changing the floorplate and fitting the follower spring with a resistance insert at its base.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Third-generation_models">Third-generation models</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glock&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Third-generation models">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In 1998, the frame was further modified with an accessory rail (called the "Universal Glock rail") to allow the mounting of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Laser_applications#Laser_sight" title="Laser applications">laser sights</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tactical_light" title="Tactical light">tactical lights</a>, and other accessories. Thumb rests on both sides of the frame and finger grooves on the front strap were added. Glock pistols with these upgrades are informally referred to as (early) "third-generation" models. Later third-generation models additionally featured a modified extractor that serves as a <a href="/wiki/Safety_(firearms)#Loaded_chamber_indicator" title="Safety (firearms)">loaded chamber indicator</a>, and the locking block was enlarged, along with the addition of an extra cross pin to aid the distribution of forces exerted by the locking block. This cross pin is known as the locking block pin and is located above the trigger pin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wo.C5.BAniak_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-wo.C5.BAniak-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<p>The polymer frames of third-generation models can be black, flat dark earth, or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Olive_drab" title="Olive drab">olive drab</a>. Besides that, non-firing dummy pistols ("P" models) and non-firing dummy pistols with resetting triggers ("R" models) have a bright red frame and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Simunition&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Simunition (page does not exist)">Simunition</a>-adapted practice pistols ("T" models) – a bright blue frame for easy identification.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2009, the Glock 22 RTF2 (Rough Textured Frame 2) (chambered in .40 S&amp;W) was introduced. This pistol featured a new checkering texture around the grip and new scalloped (fish gill-shaped) serrations at the rear of the sides of the slide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> Many of the existing models became available in the RTF2 version, including the 31, 32, 23, 21, 19. Some of those did not have the fish gills.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Fourth-generation_models">Fourth-generation models</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glock&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Fourth-generation models">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gen4grip.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="1024" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Gen4grip.jpg/220px-Gen4grip.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Gen4grip.jpg/330px-Gen4grip.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Gen4grip.jpg/440px-Gen4grip.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gen4grip.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Comparison of "third-" (left) and "fourth-generation" (right) Glock 19 grip frames</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Glock_17_MOD_45154998.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1934" data-file-width="3000" height="142" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Glock_17_MOD_45154998.jpg/220px-Glock_17_MOD_45154998.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Glock_17_MOD_45154998.jpg/330px-Glock_17_MOD_45154998.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Glock_17_MOD_45154998.jpg/440px-Glock_17_MOD_45154998.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Glock_17_MOD_45154998.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Glock 17 Gen4 as issued by the British Armed Forces under the L131A1 General Service Pistol designation</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>At the 2010 <a href="/wiki/SHOT_Show" title="SHOT Show">SHOT Show</a>, Glock presented the "fourth generation", now dubbed "Gen4" by Glock itself. Updates centered on ergonomics and the recoil spring assembly. Some parts of fourth-generation Glock pistols cannot be interchanged with those of the previous generations. The initial two fourth-generation models announced were the full-sized Glock 17 and Glock 22, chambered for the 9×19 mm Parabellum and .40 S&amp;W cartridges, respectively. The pistols were displayed with a modified rough-textured frame, grip checkering, and interchangeable backstraps of different sizes. "Gen4" is rollmarked on the slide next to the model number to identify the fourth-generation pistols.</p>
<p>The basic grip size of the fourth-generation Glock pistols is slightly smaller compared to the previous design. A punch is provided to remove the standard trigger housing pin and replace it for the longer cross pin needed to mount the medium or large backstrap that will increase the trigger distance by 2 mm (0.079 in) or 4 mm (0.16 in). With the medium backstrap installed, the grip size is identical to the third-generation pistols. The magazine release catches are enlarged and reversible for left-handed use. To use the exchangeable magazine release feature, fourth-generation Glock magazines have two notches cut on both sides of the magazine body.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-us.glock.com_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-us.glock.com-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:No_original_research#Primary.2C_secondary_and_tertiary_sources" title="Wikipedia:No original research"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable secondary sources. (October 2015)">non-primary source needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Mechanically, fourth-generation Glock pistols are fitted with a dual recoil spring assembly to help reduce perceived recoil and increase service life expectancy. Earlier subcompact Glock models such as the Glock 26 and Glock 30 have already used a dual recoil spring assembly which was carried over to the fourth-generation versions of those models. The slide and barrel shelf have been resized, and the front portion of the polymer frame has been widened and internally enlarged, to accommodate the dual recoil spring assembly. The trigger mechanism housing has also been modified to fit into the smaller-sized grip space.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<p>The introduction of fourth-generation Glock pistols continued in July 2010 when the Glock 19 and Glock 23, the reduced size "compact" versions of the Glock 17 and Glock 22, became available for retail.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> In late 2010, Glock continued the introduction of fourth-generation models with the Glock 26 and Glock 27 "subcompact" variants.</p>
<p>In January 2013, more fourth-generation Glock pistols were introduced commercially during the annual SHOT Show, including the Glock 20 Generation 4 along with other fourth-generation Glock models.</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="2011_recoil_spring_assembly_exchange_program">2011 recoil spring assembly exchange program</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glock&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: 2011 recoil spring assembly exchange program">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>In September 2011, Glock announced a recoil spring exchange program in which the manufacturer voluntarily offers to exchange the recoil spring assemblies of its fourth-generation pistols (with the exception of the "subcompact" Glock 26 and Glock 27 models) sold before 22 July 2011 at no cost "to ensure our products perform up to GLOCK’s stringent standards", according to the company.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="M_series">M series</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glock&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: M series">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>On 29 June 2016 the United States Federal Bureau of Investigation (FBI) awarded a contract to Glock to provide new 9×19mm Parabellum chambered duty pistols.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> The solicitation specifications deviated from the specifications of Glock fourth-generation models.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup></p>
<p>In August 2016 the Indianapolis Metro Police Department (IMPD) started training with a batch of Glock 17M pistols. The most obvious difference with the Glock third and fourth-generation models on published images is the omission of finger grooves on the grip.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> The IMPD issued a Glock 17M voluntary recall following failures encountered while dry firing the pistols during training. According to Major Riddle with the IMPD; "Glock is working to correct the problem and we hope to begin issuing the new [17Ms] as soon as December".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Design_details">Design details</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glock&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Design details">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Operating_mechanism">Operating mechanism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glock&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Operating mechanism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Glock 17 is a <a href="/wiki/9%C3%9719mm_Parabellum" title="9×19mm Parabellum">9 mm</a> <a href="/wiki/Recoil_operation#Short_recoil_operation" title="Recoil operation">short recoil</a>–operated, locked-breech <a href="/wiki/Semi-automatic_pistol" title="Semi-automatic pistol">semi-automatic pistol</a> that uses a modified <a href="/wiki/John_Browning" title="John Browning">Browning</a> cam-lock system adapted from the <a href="/wiki/Browning_Hi-Power" title="Browning Hi-Power">Hi-Power</a> pistol.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sweeney2008-p23_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sweeney2008-p23-38">[38]</a></sup> The firearm's locking mechanism uses a linkless, vertically tilting barrel with a rectangular breech that locks into the ejection port cut-out in the <a href="/wiki/Pistol_slide" title="Pistol slide">slide</a>. During the recoil stroke, the barrel moves rearward initially locked together with the slide about 3 mm (0.12 in) until the bullet leaves the barrel and chamber pressure drops to a safe level. A ramped lug extension at the base of the barrel then interacts with a tapered locking block integrated into the frame, forcing the barrel down and unlocking it from the slide. This camming action terminates the barrel's movement while the slide continues back under recoil, extracting and ejecting the spent cartridge casing. The slide's uninterrupted rearward movement and counter-recoil cycle are characteristic of the Browning system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kokalis_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kokalis-39">[39]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Features">Features</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glock&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Features">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Glock_30-JH02.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1800" data-file-width="2553" height="155" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Glock_30-JH02.jpg/220px-Glock_30-JH02.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Glock_30-JH02.jpg/330px-Glock_30-JH02.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Glock_30-JH02.jpg/440px-Glock_30-JH02.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Glock_30-JH02.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A subcompact Glock 30 field stripped to its main parts with a <a href="/wiki/.45_ACP" title=".45 ACP">.45 ACP</a> round</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The slide features a spring-loaded claw <a href="/wiki/Extractor_(firearms)" title="Extractor (firearms)">extractor</a>, and the stamped <a href="/wiki/Sheet_metal" title="Sheet metal">sheet metal</a> ejector is pinned to the trigger mechanism housing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> Pistols after 2002 have a reshaped extractor that serves as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Loaded_chamber_indicator" title="Loaded chamber indicator">loaded chamber indicator</a>. When a cartridge is present in the chamber, a tactile metal edge protrudes slightly out immediately behind the ejection port on the right side of the slide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:No_original_research#Primary.2C_secondary_and_tertiary_sources" title="Wikipedia:No original research"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable secondary sources. (October 2015)">non-primary source needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Trigger_(firearms)#Function" title="Trigger (firearms)">striker firing mechanism</a> has a spring-loaded <a href="/wiki/Firing_pin" title="Firing pin">firing pin</a> that is cocked in two stages that the firing pin spring powers. The factory-standard firing pin spring is rated at 24 <a href="/wiki/Newton_(unit)" title="Newton (unit)">N</a> (5.4 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pound-force" title="Pound-force">lb<sub>f</sub></a>), but by using a modified firing pin spring, it can be increased to 28 N (6.3 lb<sub>f</sub>) or to 31 N (7.0 lb<sub>f</sub>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-us.glock.com_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-us.glock.com-25">[25]</a></sup> When the pistol is charged, the firing pin is in the half-cock position. As the trigger is pulled, the firing pin is then fully cocked. At the end of its travel, the trigger bar is tilted downward by the connector, releasing the firing pin to fire the cartridge. The connector resets the trigger bar so that the firing pin will be captured in half-cock at the end of the firing cycle. This is known as a <a href="/wiki/Trigger_(firearms)#Pre-set" title="Trigger (firearms)">preset</a> trigger mechanism, referred to as the "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Safe_action" title="Safe action">Safe Action</a>" trigger by the manufacturer. The connector ensures the pistol can only fire semiautomatically.</p>
<p>The factory-standard, two-stage trigger has a trigger travel of 12.5 mm (0.49 in) and is rated at 25 <a href="/wiki/Newton_(unit)" title="Newton (unit)">N</a> (5.6 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pound-force" title="Pound-force">lb<sub>f</sub></a>), but by using a modified connector, it can be increased to 35 N (7.9 lb<sub>f</sub>) or lowered to 20 N (4.5 lb<sub>f</sub>). In response to a request made by American law enforcement agencies for a two-stage trigger with increased trigger pull, Glock introduced the NY1 (New York) trigger module, which features a flat spring in a plastic housing that replaces the trigger bar's standard coil spring. This trigger modification is available in two versions: NY1 and NY2 that are rated at 25 N (5.6 lb<sub>f</sub>) to 40 N (9.0 lb<sub>f</sub>) and 32 N (7.2 lb<sub>f</sub>) to 50 N (11.2 lb<sub>f</sub>), respectively, which require about 20 N (4.5 lb<sub>f</sub>) to 30 N (6.7 lb<sub>f</sub>) of force to disengage the safeties and another 10 N (2.2 lb<sub>f</sub>) to 20 N (4.5 lb<sub>f</sub>) in the second stage to fire a shot.</p>
<p>The Glock's frame, magazine body, and several other components are made from a high-strength <a href="/wiki/Nylon" title="Nylon">nylon</a>-based <a href="/wiki/Polymer" title="Polymer">polymer</a> invented by Gaston Glock, called Polymer 2.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kasler_136_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kasler_136-42">[42]</a></sup> This plastic was specially formulated to provide increased durability and is more resilient than <a href="/wiki/Carbon_steel" title="Carbon steel">carbon steel</a> and most <a href="/wiki/Alloy_steel" title="Alloy steel">steel alloys</a>. Polymer 2 is resistant to shock, <a href="/wiki/Corrosive_substance" title="Corrosive substance">caustic liquids</a>, and temperature extremes where traditional steel/alloy frames would warp and become brittle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kasler_136_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kasler_136-42">[42]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Injection_molding" title="Injection molding">injection-molded</a> frame contains four hardened steel guide rails for the slide: two at the rear of the frame, and the remaining pair above and in front of the trigger guard. The trigger guard itself is squared off at the front and checkered. The grip has an angle of 109° and a nonslip, <a href="/wiki/Stippling#Other_uses" title="Stippling">stippled</a> surface on the sides and both the front and rear straps.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> The frame houses the locking block, which is an <a href="/wiki/Investment_casting" title="Investment casting">investment casting</a> that engages a 45° camming surface on the barrel's lower camming lug. It is retained in the frame by a steel axis pin that holds the trigger and slide catch. The trigger housing is held to the frame by means of a polymer pin. A spring-loaded sheet-metal pressing serves as the slide catch, which is secured from unintentional manipulation by a raised guard molded into the frame.</p>
<p>The Glock pistol has a relatively low slide profile, which holds the barrel axis close to the shooter's hand and makes the pistol more comfortable to fire by reducing <a href="/wiki/Muzzle_rise" title="Muzzle rise">muzzle rise</a> and allows for faster aim recovery in rapid firing sequences. The rectangular slide is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Milling_machine" title="Milling machine">milled</a> from a single block of ordnance-grade steel using <a href="/wiki/Numerical_control" title="Numerical control">CNC</a> machinery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kokalis_321_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kokalis_321-44">[44]</a></sup> The barrel and slide undergo two hardening processes prior to treatment with a proprietary <a href="/wiki/Nitriding" title="Nitriding">nitriding</a> process called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tenifer" title="Tenifer">Tenifer</a>. The Tenifer treatment is applied in a 500 °C (932 °F) nitrate bath.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kasler_136_42-2"><a href="#cite_note-Kasler_136-42">[42]</a></sup> The Tenifer finish is between 0.04 and 0.05 mm (0.0016 and 0.0020 in) in thickness, and is characterized by extreme resistance to wear and corrosion; it penetrates the metal, and treated parts have similar properties even below the surface to a certain depth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kasler_137_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kasler_137-45">[45]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Tenifer process produces a matte gray-colored, nonglare surface with a 64 <a href="/wiki/Rockwell_scale" title="Rockwell scale">Rockwell C</a> hardness rating and a 99% resistance to salt water corrosion (which meets or exceeds <a href="/wiki/Stainless_steel" title="Stainless steel">stainless steel</a> specifications),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kokalis_321_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kokalis_321-44">[44]</a></sup> making the Glock particularly suitable for individuals carrying the pistol <a href="/wiki/Concealed_carry_in_the_United_States" title="Concealed carry in the United States">concealed</a> as the highly chloride-resistant finish allows the pistol to better endure the effects of perspiration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kasler_137_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kasler_137-45">[45]</a></sup> Glock steel parts using the Tenifer treatment are more corrosion resistant than analogous gun parts having other finishes or treatments, including <a href="/wiki/Polytetrafluoroethylene" title="Polytetrafluoroethylene">Teflon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bluing_(steel)" title="Bluing (steel)">bluing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chrome_plating#industrial_chrome" title="Chrome plating">hard chrome plating</a>, or phosphates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kasler_137_45-2"><a href="#cite_note-Kasler_137-45">[45]</a></sup> During 2010 Glock switched from the salt bath nitriding Tenifer process to a not exactly disclosed <a href="/wiki/Ferritic_nitrocarburizing#Gaseous_ferritic_nitrocarburizing" title="Ferritic nitrocarburizing">gas nitriding</a> process. After applying the nitriding process, a black <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parkerized" title="Parkerized">Parkerized</a> decorative surface finish is applied. The underlying nitriding treatment will remain, protecting these parts even if the decorative surface finish were to wear off.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MetalTreatments_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-MetalTreatments-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<p>A current production Glock 17 consists of 34 parts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-us.glock.com_25-2"><a href="#cite_note-us.glock.com-25">[25]</a></sup> For maintenance, the pistol disassembles into five main groups: the barrel, slide, frame, magazine, and recoil-spring assembly. The firearm is designed for the <a href="/wiki/NATO" title="NATO">NATO</a>-standard 9×19mm Parabellum <a href="/wiki/Handgun" title="Handgun">pistol</a> <a href="/wiki/Cartridge_(firearms)" title="Cartridge (firearms)">cartridge</a>, but can use high-power (increased pressure) +P and +P+ ammunition with either full-metal-jacket or jacketed hollow-point projectiles.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Barrel">Barrel</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glock&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Barrel">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Glock_17_9mmPara_004.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="691" data-file-width="442" height="344" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Glock_17_9mmPara_004.jpg/220px-Glock_17_9mmPara_004.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Glock_17_9mmPara_004.jpg/330px-Glock_17_9mmPara_004.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Glock_17_9mmPara_004.jpg/440px-Glock_17_9mmPara_004.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Glock_17_9mmPara_004.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Standard sighting arrangement of a "first-generation" Glock 17</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The hammer-forged barrel has a female type <a href="/wiki/Polygonal_rifling" title="Polygonal rifling">polygonal rifling</a> with a right-hand twist. The stabilization of the round is not by conventional <a href="/wiki/Rifling" title="Rifling">rifling</a>, using lands and grooves, but rather through a polygonal profile consisting of a series of six or eight interconnected noncircular segments (only the .45 ACP and .45 GAP have octagonal polygonal rifling). Each depressed segment within the interior of the barrel is the equivalent of a groove in a conventional barrel. Thus, the interior of the barrel consists of smooth arcs of steel rather than sharply defined slots.</p>
<p>The method by which Glock barrels are rifled is somewhat unusual; instead of using a traditional <a href="/wiki/Broaching_(metalworking)" title="Broaching (metalworking)">broaching machine</a> to cut the rifling into the bore, the Glock process involves beating a slowly rotating <a href="/wiki/Mandrel" title="Mandrel">mandrel</a> through the bore to obtain the <a href="/wiki/Hexagon" title="Hexagon">hexagonal</a> or <a href="/wiki/Octagon" title="Octagon">octagonal</a> shape.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kasler_138_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kasler_138-46">[46]</a></sup> As a result, the barrel's thickness in the area of each groove is not compromised as with conventional square-cut barrels. This has the advantage of providing a better gas seal around the projectile as the bore has a slightly smaller diameter, which translates into more efficient use of the combustion gases trapped behind the bullet,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kasler_138_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kasler_138-46">[46]</a></sup> slightly greater (consistency in) muzzle velocities, and increased accuracy and ease of maintenance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barrel_History_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-Barrel_History-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Safety">Safety</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glock&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Safety">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Glock pistols are designed with three independent safety mechanisms to prevent accidental discharge. The system, designated "Safe Action" by Glock, consists of an external <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trigger_safety" title="Trigger safety">integrated trigger safety</a> and two automatic internal safeties: a firing pin safety, and a drop safety.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> The external safety is a small inner lever contained in the trigger. Pressing the lever activates the trigger bar and sheet metal connector. The firing pin safety is a solid hardened steel pin that, in the secured state, blocks the firing pin channel (disabling the firing pin in its longitudinal axis). It is pushed upward to release the firing pin for firing only when the trigger is actuated and the safety is pushed up through the backward movement of the trigger bar. The drop safety guides the trigger bar in a ramp that is released only when direct rearward pressure is applied to the trigger. The three safety mechanisms are automatically disengaged one after the other when the trigger is squeezed, and are automatically reactivated when the trigger is released.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wo.C5.BAniak_17-2"><a href="#cite_note-Wo.C5.BAniak-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> This passive safety system omits the manipulation of traditional on-off levers, hammers, or other external safeties as found in many other handgun designs. The ability to fire immediately, without worrying about an external safety, is one feature Glock has stressed as an advantage when selling its guns, especially to police departments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bloom-safety_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-bloom-safety-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2003, Glock announced the Internal Locking System (ILS) safety feature. The ILS is a manually activated lock located in the back of the pistol's grip. It is cylindrical in design and, according to Glock, each key is unique. When activated, the lock causes a tab to protrude from the rear of the grip, giving both a visual and tactile indication as to whether the lock is engaged or not. When activated, the ILS renders the Glock unfireable, as well as making it impossible to disassemble. When disengaged, the ILS adds no further safety mechanisms to the Glock pistol. The ILS is available as an option on most Glock pistols. Glock pistols cannot be retrofitted to accommodate the ILS. The lock must be factory built in Austria and shipped as a special order.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Feeding">Feeding</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glock&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Feeding">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Glock 17 feeds from staggered-column or double stack <a href="/wiki/Magazine_(firearms)" title="Magazine (firearms)">magazines</a> that have a 17-round capacity (which can be extended to 19 with an optional floor plate) or optional 33-round <a href="/wiki/Magazine_(firearms)#High-capacity_magazines" title="Magazine (firearms)">high-capacity magazines</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> For jurisdictions which restrict magazine capacity to 10 rounds, Glock offers single-stack, 10-round magazines. The magazines are made of steel and are overmolded with plastic. A steel spring drives a plastic follower. After the last cartridge has been fired, the slide remains open on the slide stop. The slide stop release lever is located on the left side of the frame directly beneath the slide and can be manipulated by the thumb of the right-handed shooter.</p>
<p>Glock magazines are interchangeable between models of the same caliber, meaning that a compact or subcompact pistol will accept magazines designed for the larger pistols chambered for the same round. However, magazines designed for compact and subcompact models will not function in larger pistols because they are not tall enough to reach the slide and magazine release. For example, the subcompact Glock 26 will accept magazines from both the full-size Glock 17 and the compact Glock 19, but the Glock 17 will not accept magazines from the smaller Glock 19 or the Glock 26. The magazines for the Glock 36, the Glock 42, and the Glock 43 are all unique; they cannot use magazines intended for another model, nor can their magazines be used in other models.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sights">Sights</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glock&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Sights">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Glock 17 has a fixed polymer combat-type <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iron_sight" title="Iron sight">sighting arrangement</a> that consists of a ramped front sight and a notched rear sight with white contrast elements painted on for increased acquisition speed – a white dot on the front post and a rectangular border on the rear notch. The rear sight can be adjusted for windage (on certain models due to the windage sights not coming as factory default), as it has a degree of lateral movement in the dovetail it is mounted in. Three other factory rear sight configurations are available in addition to the standard 6.5 mm (0.26 in) height sight: a lower impact 6.1 mm (0.24 in) sight, and two higher impact versions – 6.9 mm (0.27 in) and 7.3 mm (0.29 in).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kokalis_322_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kokalis_322-52">[52]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Accessories">Accessories</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glock&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Accessories">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;max-width:312px">
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:102px;max-width:102px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Glock34_with_gtl22.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2304" data-file-width="3456" height="67" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Glock34_with_gtl22.jpg/100px-Glock34_with_gtl22.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Glock34_with_gtl22.jpg/150px-Glock34_with_gtl22.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Glock34_with_gtl22.jpg/200px-Glock34_with_gtl22.jpg 2x" width="100"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Glock 34 with a GTL 22 attachment featuring a dimmable xenon white light and a red laser</div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:102px;max-width:102px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:D150211dv1047.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2712" data-file-width="4068" height="67" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/D150211dv1047.jpg/100px-D150211dv1047.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/D150211dv1047.jpg/150px-D150211dv1047.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/D150211dv1047.jpg/200px-D150211dv1047.jpg 2x" width="100"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">A military diver displaying a Glock 17 fitted with maritime spring cups</div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:102px;max-width:102px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:GlockOrig.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="441" data-file-width="593" height="74" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/GlockOrig.jpg/100px-GlockOrig.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/GlockOrig.jpg/150px-GlockOrig.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/GlockOrig.jpg/200px-GlockOrig.jpg 2x" width="100"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Polymer holster for Glock pistols</div>
</div>
<div style="clear:left"></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Glock pistol accessories available from the factory include several devices for tactical illumination, such as a series of front rail-mounted "Glock tactical lights" featuring a white <a href="/wiki/Tactical_light" title="Tactical light">tactical light</a> and an optional visible laser sight. An alternate version of the tactical light using an invisible <a href="/wiki/Infrared" title="Infrared">infrared</a> light and laser sight is available, designed to be used with an infrared <a href="/wiki/Night_vision_device" title="Night vision device">night vision device</a>. Another lighting accessory is an adapter to mount a flashlight onto the bottom of a magazine.</p>
<p>Polymer holsters in various configurations and matching magazine pouches are available. In addition, Glock produces optional triggers, recoil springs, slide stops, magazine release levers, and maritime spring cups. Maritime spring cups are designed to allow the pistol to be fired immediately after being submerged in water. They feature additional openings that allow liquids to flow and escape around them, offering enhanced reliability when water has penetrated into the firing pin assembly channel.</p>
<p>Magazine floor plates (or +2 baseplates), which expand the capacity of the standard magazines by two rounds are available for models chambered for the 9×19mm Parabellum, <a href="/wiki/.40_S%26W" title=".40 S&amp;W">.40 S&amp;W</a>, <a href="/wiki/.357_SIG" title=".357 SIG">.357 SIG</a>, and <a href="/wiki/.380_ACP" title=".380 ACP">.380 ACP</a> cartridges. In addition to the standard nonadjustable polymer sight line, three alternative sight lines are offered by Glock. These consist of steel, adjustable, and self-illuminating <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tritium_sights" title="Tritium sights">tritium</a> night rear sights and factory steel and self-illuminating tritium contrast pointer steel front sights.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Variants">Variants</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glock&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Variants">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Following the introduction of the Glock 17, numerous variants and versions have been offered. Variants that differ in caliber, frame, and slide length are identified by different model numbers with the exception of the Glock 17L. Other changes not dealing with frame and slide length are identified with suffixes, such as "C", which denotes <a href="/wiki/Muzzle_brake" title="Muzzle brake">compensated</a> models.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:No_original_research#Primary.2C_secondary_and_tertiary_sources" title="Wikipedia:No original research"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable secondary sources. (October 2015)">non-primary source needed</span></a></i>]</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> Minor options such as frame color, sights, and included accessories are identified by a separate model code on the box and do not appear anywhere on the firearm.</p>
<p>Glock pistols are made in five form factors, all modeled after the original full-sized Glock 17. "Standard" models are designed as full-sized duty firearms with a large magazine capacity. "Compact" models are slightly smaller with reduced magazine capacity and lighter weight, while maintaining a usable grip length. "Subcompact" models are designed for easier carry, and being lighter and shorter, are intended to be used with two fingers on the grip below the trigger guard, and lack an accessory rail like the larger, after generation two, Glock models. The .45 ACP and <a href="/wiki/10mm_Auto" title="10mm Auto">10mm Auto</a> models have bigger, wider slides and are slightly larger than the smaller-chambered pistols and are available in the subcompact models Glock 29 (10mm) and Glock 30 (.45 ACP). Glock produces a single-stack "Slimline" .45 ACP pistol, the Glock 36. "Competition" versions have longer barrels and slides, adjustable sights, an extended slide and magazine release.</p>
<p>Beginning in 2007, Glock introduced several "Short Frame" models designated by the suffix "SF". The short frame was originally designed to compete in the now cancelled U.S. military <a href="/wiki/Joint_Combat_Pistol" title="Joint Combat Pistol">Joint Combat Pistol</a> trials for a new .45 ACP pistol to replace the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/M9_pistol" title="M9 pistol">M9 pistol</a>. Glock's entry featured an optional ambidextrous magazine release and <a href="/wiki/Picatinny_rail" title="Picatinny rail">MIL-STD-1913</a> rail along with a reduction in the size of the backstrap. The Glock 21SF is currently available in three versions: one with a Picatinny rail and ambidextrous magazine release and two with a Universal Glock rail available with or without the ambidextrous magazine release. Current 10 mm and .45 ACP Glock magazines are manufactured with ambidextrous magazine release cutouts. As of January 2009, the Glock 20, 21, 29, and 30 were offered in short-framed variations. These models incorporate a 2.5 mm (0.098 in) reduction in trigger reach, and full-sized models feature a 4 mm (0.16 in) reduction in heel depth, which corresponds to an overall reduction in length for those models.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="9.C3.9719mm_Parabellum">9×19mm Parabellum</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glock&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: 9×19mm Parabellum">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul>
<li>The <b>Glock 17</b><span id="Glock_17"></span> is the original 9×19mm Parabellum model, with a standard magazine capacity of 17 rounds. Several modified versions of the Glock 17 have been introduced.
<ul>
<li>The Glock 17C, introduced in 1996, incorporates slots cut in the barrel and slide to compensate for muzzle rise and recoil. Many other Glock pistols now come with this option, all with a "C" suffix on the slide.</li>
<li>The Glock 17L, introduced in 1988, incorporates a longer slide and extended barrel. Initially, the Glock 17L had three holes in the top of the barrel and a corresponding slot in the slide; however, later production pistols lack the holes in the barrel. The Glock 17L is manufactured in limited quantities.</li>
<li>The Glock 17MB is a version with ambidextrous magazine catch. This model, along with the other MB variants, was discontinued upon the introduction of the fourth-generation models, which features a reversible magazine catch.</li>
</ul>
</li>
</ul>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MarineCorpsGlock18.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="900" data-file-width="1200" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/MarineCorpsGlock18.jpg/220px-MarineCorpsGlock18.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/MarineCorpsGlock18.jpg/330px-MarineCorpsGlock18.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/MarineCorpsGlock18.jpg/440px-MarineCorpsGlock18.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:MarineCorpsGlock18.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Glock 18, chambered in 9×19mm Parabellum, fitted with a detachable shoulder stock being fired in fully automatic mode</div>
</div>
</div>
<ul>
<li>The <b>Glock 18</b><span id="Glock_18"></span> is a <a href="/wiki/Selective_fire" title="Selective fire">selective-fire</a> variant of the Glock 17, developed at the request of the Austrian <a href="/wiki/Counter-terrorism" title="Counter-terrorism">counter-terrorist</a> unit <a href="/wiki/EKO_Cobra" title="EKO Cobra">EKO Cobra</a>. Originally produced in 1986, this <a href="/wiki/Machine_pistol" title="Machine pistol">machine pistol</a>–class firearm has a lever-type fire-control selector switch, installed on the serrated portion of the rear left side of the slide. With the selector lever in the bottom position, the pistol fires <a href="/wiki/Automatic_firearm" title="Automatic firearm">fully automatically</a>, and with the selector lever in the top position, the pistol fires semiautomatically. The firearm is typically used with an extended 33-round-capacity magazine, although other magazines from the Glock 17 will function, with available capacities of 10, 17, or 19 rounds. Early Glock 18 models were ported to reduce <a href="/wiki/Muzzle_rise" title="Muzzle rise">muzzle rise</a> during automatic fire. Another compensated variant was produced, known as the Glock 18C. It has a keyhole opening cut into the forward portion of the slide, similar to the opening on the Glock long-slide models, although the Glock 18 has a standard-length slide. The keyhole opening provides an area to allow the four, progressively larger (from back to front) compensator cuts machined into the barrel to vent the propellant gases upwards, affording more control over the rapid-firing machine pistol.
<ul>
<li>The compensator cuts start about halfway back on the top of the barrel. The two rear cuts are narrower than the two front cuts. The slide is hollowed, or dished-out, in a rectangular pattern between the rear of the ejection port and the rear sight. The rate of fire in fully automatic mode is around 1,100–1,200 rounds per minute. Most of the other characteristics are equivalent to the Glock 17, although the slide, frame, and certain fire-control parts of the Glock 18 are not interchangeable with other Glock models.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
</li>
</ul>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:GLOCK_19.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1327" data-file-width="1940" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/GLOCK_19.JPG/220px-GLOCK_19.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/GLOCK_19.JPG/330px-GLOCK_19.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/GLOCK_19.JPG/440px-GLOCK_19.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:GLOCK_19.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The compact Glock 19 in 9×19mm Parabellum</div>
</div>
</div>
<ul>
<li>The <b>Glock 19</b><span id="Glock_19"></span> is effectively a reduced-size Glock 17, called the "Compact" by the manufacturer. It was first produced in 1988, primarily for military and law enforcement. The Glock 19's barrel and pistol grip are shorter by about 12 mm (0.5 in) compared to the Glock 17 and uses a magazine with a standard capacity of 15 rounds. The pistol is compatible with factory magazines from the Glock 17 and Glock 18, giving the Glock 19 available capacities of: 17 rounds (standard magazine with +2 extension), 10, 17, and 19 (standard Glock 17 magazine with +2), and the 31 (standard Glock 18 magazine with +2 removed) and 33 rounds of the Glock 18. To preserve the operational reliability of the short recoil system, the mass of the slide remains the same as in the Glock 17 from which it is derived. With the exception of the slide, frame, barrel, locking block, recoil spring, guide rod, and slide lock spring, all of the other components are interchangeable between the models 17 and 19.</li>
</ul>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Glock_26.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="534" data-file-width="800" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Glock_26.JPG/220px-Glock_26.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Glock_26.JPG/330px-Glock_26.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Glock_26.JPG/440px-Glock_26.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Glock_26.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The subcompact Glock 26 with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tritium_illumination#Small_arms_sights" title="Tritium illumination">tritium night sights</a> in 9×19mm Parabellum</div>
</div>
</div>
<ul>
<li>The <b>Glock 26</b><span id="Glock_26"></span> is a 9×19mm "subcompact" variant designed for <a href="/wiki/Concealed_carry_in_the_United_States" title="Concealed carry in the United States">concealed carry</a> and was introduced in 1995, mainly for the civilian market. It features a smaller frame compared to the Glock 19, with a pistol grip that supports only two fingers, a shorter barrel and slide, and a double-stack magazine with a standard capacity of 10 rounds. A factory magazine with a +2 extension gives a capacity of 12 rounds. In addition, factory magazines from the Glock 17, Glock 18, and Glock 19, with capacities of 15, 17, 19, 31 and 33 rounds, will function in the Glock 26. More than simply a "shortened" Glock 19, design of the subcompact Glock 26 required extensive rework of the frame, locking block, and spring assembly that features a dual recoil spring.</li>
<li>The <b>Glock 34</b><span id="Glock_34"></span> is a competition version of the Glock 17. It is similar to its predecessor, the Glock 17L, but with a slightly shorter slide and barrel, to meet the maximum size requirements for many sanctioned action pistol sporting events. It was developed and produced in 1998, and compared to the Glock 17, features a 21 mm (0.8 in) longer barrel and slide. It has an extended magazine release, extended slide stop lever, 20 <a href="/wiki/Newton_(unit)" title="Newton (unit)">N</a> (4.5 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pound-force" title="Pound-force">lb<sub>f</sub></a>) trigger pull, and an adjustable rear sight. The sides at the front of the slide are slanted instead of squared. Further, the top of the slide and parts of its inside are milled out, creating a conspicuous hole at the top designed to reduce front-end muzzle weight to better balance the pistol and reduce the overall weight of the slide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup></li>
<li>The <b>Glock 43</b><span id="Glock_43"></span> is a "slimline" version of the subcompact Glock 26 that features an ultracompact slide and frame. The Glock 43 is the first Glock pistol to be manufactured with a single-stack 9×19mm Parabellum magazine, having a standard capacity of six rounds and being unique to the model. Unlike other subcompact Glock pistols, the Glock 43 cannot use factory magazines from its larger relatives due to its single-stack magazine design. It also does not allow you to remove the backplate grip like on the 4th gen Glocks.</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="10mm_Auto">10mm Auto</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glock&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: 10mm Auto">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:GLOCK_29_10mm.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="530" data-file-width="779" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/GLOCK_29_10mm.jpg/220px-GLOCK_29_10mm.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/GLOCK_29_10mm.jpg/330px-GLOCK_29_10mm.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/GLOCK_29_10mm.jpg/440px-GLOCK_29_10mm.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:GLOCK_29_10mm.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The subcompact third-generation Glock 29 in 10mm Auto</div>
</div>
</div>
<ul>
<li>The <b>Glock 20</b><span id="Glock_20"></span>, introduced in 1991, was developed for the then-growing law enforcement and security forces market for the 10mm Auto. The pistol handles both full-power and reduced "FBI" loads that have reduced muzzle velocity. Due to the longer cartridge and higher pressures, the pistol is slightly larger than the Glock 17, having a roughly 2.5 mm (0.1 in) greater width and 7 mm (0.3 in) greater length. Though many small parts interchange with the Glock 17, with a close to 50% parts commonality, the major assemblies are scaled-up and do not interchange. The standard magazine capacity of the Glock 20 is 15 rounds. In 2009, Glock announced they would offer a 152 mm (6.0 in) barrel as a drop-in option.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup>
<ul>
<li>The Glock 20SF is a version of the Glock 20 that uses the Short Frame (SF) which is based on the standard G20 frame (same width), but reduces the trigger reach from the back of the grip by 2.5 mm (0.098 in) and the heel of the pistol is shortened by 4 mm (0.16 in) so the trigger can be reached and operated better by users with relatively small hands.</li>
</ul>
</li>
<li>The <b>Glock 29</b><span id="Glock_29"></span> is a 10mm Auto equivalent of the subcompact Glock 26 introduced in 1997 along with the Glock 30 (.45 ACP). The pistol features a 96 mm (3.8 in) barrel and a standard magazine capacity of 10 rounds. Like other subcompact Glock pistols, the Glock 29 functions with the factory magazines from its related full-size model, giving an optional capacity of 15 rounds.
<ul>
<li>The Glock 29SF version of the Glock 29 uses the SFwhich is based on the standard G29 frame (same width), but reduces the trigger reach from the back of the grip by 2.5 mm (0.098 in).</li>
</ul>
</li>
<li>The <b>Glock 40</b><span id="Glock_40"></span>, introduced in 2015, is a 10mm Auto equivalent of the long-slide Glock 17L. The Glock 40 is only made with the "Gen4" frame and "MOS" (Modular Optic System) configuration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Manning2015_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-Manning2015-62">[62]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id=".45_ACP">.45 ACP</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glock&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: .45 ACP">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Glock_36.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1225" data-file-width="1691" height="159" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Glock_36.JPG/220px-Glock_36.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Glock_36.JPG/330px-Glock_36.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Glock_36.JPG/440px-Glock_36.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Glock_36.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The slim-frame Glock 36 in .45 ACP</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Glock pistols chambered for the .45 ACP (and the <a href="/wiki/.45_GAP" title=".45 GAP">.45 GAP</a>) feature <a href="/wiki/Octagon" title="Octagon">octagonal</a> <a href="/wiki/Polygonal_rifling" title="Polygonal rifling">polygonal rifling</a> rather than the hexagonal shaped bores used for models in most other chamberings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Glock_FAQ_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Glock_FAQ-63">[63]</a></sup> Octagonal rifling provides a better gas seal in relatively large diameter rifled bores, since an octagon resembles a <a href="/wiki/Circle" title="Circle">circle</a> more closely than a hexagon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barrel_History_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-Barrel_History-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li>The <b>Glock 21</b><span id="Glock_21"></span> is a .45 ACP version of the Glock 20 designed primarily for the American market.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> Compared to the Glock 20 chambered in 10mm Auto, the slide of the Glock 21 is lighter to compensate for the lower-energy .45 ACP cartridge. The standard Glock 21 magazine is of the single-position-feed, staggered-column type with a capacity of 13 rounds.
<ul>
<li>The Glock 21SF is a version of the Glock 21 that uses a Short Frame lower which is based on the standard G21 frame (same width), but reduces trigger reach from the back of the grip by 2.5 mm (0.098 in), and the heel of the pistol is shortened by 4 mm (0.16 in) so the trigger can be reached and operated better by users with smaller hands.</li>
</ul>
</li>
<li>The <b>Glock 30</b><span id="Glock_30"></span> is a .45 ACP version of the subcompact Glock 29, with a standard magazine capacity of 10 rounds. The factory magazine from the Glock 21, with a capacity of 13 rounds, will function in the Glock 30.
<ul>
<li>The Glock 30SF is a version of the Glock 30 that uses a Short Frame lower which is based on the standard G30 frame (same width), but reduces trigger reach from the back of the grip by 2.5 mm (0.098 in). The G30SF accepts the same double-stack .45ACP magazines as the G30 and G21.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McNab2015_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-McNab2015-65">[65]</a></sup></li>
<li>The Glock 30S is a version of the Glock 30 that features a thin slide (same slide as the G36), a Short Frame lower, and a double stack magazine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> Like the G30, G30S magazines holds 10 rounds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
</li>
<li>The <b>Glock 36</b><span id="Glock_36"></span> is a "slimline" version of the subcompact Glock 30 that features an ultracompact slide and frame and is chambered for the .45 ACP cartridge. The Glock 36 is the first Glock pistol to be manufactured with a single-stack magazine, having a standard capacity of six rounds and being unique to the model. The Glock 36 cannot use factory magazines from its larger relatives due to its single-stack magazine design.</li>
<li>The <b>Glock 41</b><span id="Glock_41"></span> is a competition version of the Glock 21, much like what the G34 is in relation to the G17; it features a 5.3-inch barrel and an elongated slide. The Glock 41 is only made with the "Gen4" frame.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id=".40_S.26W">.40 S&amp;W</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glock&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: .40 S&amp;W">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Glock22inOliveDrab.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="498" data-file-width="700" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Glock22inOliveDrab.jpg/220px-Glock22inOliveDrab.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Glock22inOliveDrab.jpg/330px-Glock22inOliveDrab.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Glock22inOliveDrab.jpg/440px-Glock22inOliveDrab.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Glock22inOliveDrab.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Glock 22 OD in .40 S&amp;W with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Olive_drab" title="Olive drab">olive drab</a> frame</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Glock35Right.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1669" data-file-width="2735" height="134" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Glock35Right.jpg/220px-Glock35Right.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Glock35Right.jpg/330px-Glock35Right.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Glock35Right.jpg/440px-Glock35Right.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Glock35Right.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The competition-oriented Glock 35 in .40 S&amp;W</div>
</div>
</div>
<ul>
<li>The <b>Glock 22</b><span id="Glock_22"></span> is a .40 S&amp;W version of the full-sized Glock 17 introduced in 1990. The pistol uses a modified slide, frame, and barrel to account for the differences in size and power of the .40 S&amp;W cartridge. The standard magazine capacity is 15 rounds.</li>
<li>The <b>Glock 23</b><span id="Glock_23"></span> is a .40 S&amp;W version of the compact Glock 19. It is dimensionally identical to the Glock 19, but is slightly heavier and uses a modified slide, frame, .40 S&amp;W barrel, and a standard magazine capacity of 13 rounds. The factory 15-round magazine from the larger Glock 22 will function in the Glock 23.</li>
<li>The <b>Glock 24</b><span id="Glock_24"></span> is a .40 S&amp;W long-slide variant of the Glock 22, similar in concept to the Glock 17L. Additionally, a compensated, ported-barrel version designated the 24C was also produced. The Glock 24 was introduced in 1994 and officially dropped from the company's regular product lineup upon the release of the Glock 34 and 35.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> However, Glock continues to make small batch runs of the Glock 24 and 24C (as well as the similarly sized Glock 17L) at irregular intervals to satisfy consumer demand built-up for factory-new units, as shown by the release into the marketplace of new production of these models during the spring of 2010 and summer of 2011.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></li>
<li>The <b>Glock 27</b><span id="Glock_27"></span> is a .40 S&amp;W version of the subcompact Glock 26, with a standard magazine capacity of 9 rounds. The factory magazines from the larger Glock 22 and 23 will function in the Glock 27, increasing capacity to 13 or 15 rounds. Spacers are available that fit on these larger-capacity magazines themselves; they have the effect of "extending" the magazine well of the pistol, thereby improving the ergonomic feel of the pistol when the longer magazines are inserted.</li>
<li>The <b>Glock 35</b><span id="Glock_35"></span> is a .40 S&amp;W version of the competition Glock 34.</li>
</ul>
